	At the end of 2019, the team at the Positive Technologies Expert Security Center (PT ESC) discovered a new cybercrime group, which they dubbed Space Pirates. It had been active since at least 2017. The first-ever comprehensive research paper describing the group saw light in early 2022. The Space Pirates group have since stepped up attacks on Russian companies: we have come across the group frequently while investigating cyberattacks in the past year. They have hardly changed their tactics, but they have developed new tools and improved their old ones.


	The cybercriminals’ main goals are still espionage and theft of confidential information, but the group has expanded its interests and the geography of its attacks. Over the year, at least 16 organizations have been attacked in Russia and one in Serbia. Some of the new victims that we identified are Russian and Serbian government and educational institutions, private security companies, aerospace manufacturers, agricultural producers, defense, energy, and infosec companies.

<article>
<h2 id="id0">Introduction</h2>
<p>
At the end of 2019, the team at the Positive Technologies Expert Security Center (PT ESC) discovered a new cybercrime group, which they dubbed Space Pirates. It had been active since at least 2017. The first-ever comprehensive <a href="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/space-pirates-tools-and-connections/?utm_source=pt&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=space-pirates&amp;utm_content=esc-threat-intelligence" target="_blank">research paper</a> describing the group saw light in early 2022. The Space Pirates group have since stepped up attacks on Russian companies: we have come across the group frequently while investigating cyberattacks in the past year. They have hardly changed their tactics, but they have developed new tools and improved their old ones.</p>

<p>The cybercriminals’ main goals are still espionage and theft of confidential information, but the group has expanded its interests and the geography of its attacks. Over the year, at least 16 organizations have been attacked in Russia and one in Serbia. Some of the new victims that we identified are Russian and Serbian government and educational institutions, private security companies, aerospace manufacturers, agricultural producers, defense, energy, and infosec companies.</p>

<h2 id="id1">1. Investigating the network infrastructure</h2>
<p>
	 We found an Acunetix installation on one of the Space Pirates command-and-control (C&amp;C) servers, which suggested that the group exploited vulnerabilities—an attack vector we had not seen it use earlier.
</p>
 <figure> <img alt="Evidence of Acunetix being installed on a Space Pirates C&amp;C server" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/11207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 1. Evidence of Acunetix being installed on a Space Pirates C&amp;C server</figcaption> </figure>
<p>
	During our investigation, we noticed that the group was interested in PST email archives (among other targets). A configuration error on a Space Pirates C&amp;C server allowed us to scan its contents, discovering two email archives belonging to a Serbian ministry.
</p>
 <figure> <img alt="C&amp;C server with web shells and stolen data" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/21207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 2. C&amp;C server with web shells and stolen data</figcaption> </figure>
<p>
	We alerted the ministry via Serbia’s National CERT. Other contents of the server included a Godzilla web shell and an obfuscated Neo-reGeorg tunnel.
</p>
<p>
	The Space Pirates network infrastructure continues to use a small number of IP addresses as indicated by the DDNS domains. The malicious actors often reuse old website URLs by creating high-level domains, such as ruclient.dns04.com.ruclient.dns04.com.
</p>
<p>
	The group had also begun using the ShadowPad malware, something we discovered as we were tracking changes in the hacker infrastructure using our internal ScanDat automated system. An alert we received pointed to a chain of SSL certificates characteristic of ShadowPad. That chain was covered in one of our previous <a href="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/shadowpad-new-activity-from-the-winnti-group/?utm_source=pt&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=space-pirates&amp;utm_content=esc-threat-intelligence" target="_blank">reports</a>. As we continued to investigate the incident in question, we found a copy of ShadowPad used by the Space Pirates group in the client’s systems.
</p>
 <figure> <img alt="Chain of SSL certificates characteristic of ShadowPad" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/31207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 3. Chain of SSL certificates characteristic of ShadowPad</figcaption> </figure>
<h2 id="id2">2. Analysis of the malware and tools</h2>
<h3 id="id2-1">2.1. Deed RAT</h3>
<p>
	Virtually every investigation we conducted found that the group was using Deed RAT. As far as we can tell, the Space Pirates group is moving away from other backdoors. Code similarities between Deed RAT and ShadowPad, noted by <a href="https://xakep.ru/2022/07/28/chinese-malware/" target="_blank">our peers</a>, suggest that the backdoor is an evolution of ShadowPad. ShadowPad is in turn believed to be <a href="https://habr.com/ru/companies/drweb/articles/525086/" target="_blank">an evolution of PlugX</a>. Unlike ShadowPad and PlugX, though, Deed RAT has been known to be exclusive to the Space Pirates group to date.
</p>
<p>
	The backdoor is still under active development. We found a 64-bit version of Deed RAT on an infected device while investigating the incident. The structure of the main module and plugin headers is all but identical to the 32-bit version. Below is what it looks like:
</p>
	<pre>    <div class="code">
struct SectionHeader {
    DWORD VirtualSize;
    DWORD SizeOfRawData;
};

struct ModuleHeader {
    DWORD Signature; // 0xDEED4554
    DWORD ModuleId;
    DWORD EntryPoint;
    QWORD OriginalBase;
    DWORD AbsoluteOffset;
    SectionHeader Sections[3];
    DWORD RelocationsVirtualSize;
};
	    </div>
	</pre>
<p>
	The string encryption algorithm in recent versions is somewhat different. String length is no longer specified, and strings are null-terminated.
</p>
 <figure> <img alt="Original encryption algorithm, with string length explicitly stated" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/41207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 4. Original encryption algorithm, with string length explicitly stated</figcaption> </figure> <figure> <img alt="Updated decryption algorithm for null-terminated strings" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/51207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 5. Updated decryption algorithm for null-terminated strings</figcaption> </figure>
<p>
	We found computers infected with Deed RAT to contain two plugins, retrieved dynamically from the C&amp;C server. The first one is named Disk, has the identifier 0×250, and is used as a disk tool. Essentially a Windows API wrapper, Disk supports the 10 network commands described below.
</p>
 <br/>

			</p>
		</td>
	</tr>
	<tr>

			<p>
				 0х251
			</p>
		</td>
		<td>
			<p>
				 List files inside folder
			</p>
		</td>
	</tr>
	<tr>
		<td>
			<p>
				 0х252
			</p>
		</td>
		<td>
			<p>
				 List files inside folder recursively. The response returns the fields of the WIN32_FIND_DATAW structure, such as timestamp, size, attributes, and name
			</p>
		</td>
	</tr>
	<tr>
		<td>
			<p>
				 0х253
			</p>
		</td>
		<td>
			<p>

			</p>
		</td>
	</tr>
	<tr>
		<td>
			<p>

	</tr>

		</td>

			</p>
		</td>
	</tr>
	<tr>
		<td>
			<p>

			</p>
		</td>

	</tr>
	<tr>
		<td>
			<p>

		</td>
		<td>
			<p>
				 Connect network drive. The command sends a NETRESOURCEW structure
			</p>
		</td>
	</tr>
	</tbody>
	</table>
</div>
<br/>
<p>
	The other plugin is named Portmap and has the identifier 0×290. The hackers likely based it on the <a href="https://github.com/kufan/LViewer/blob/master/Client/ZXPortMap.cpp" target="_blank">ZXPortMap</a> utility often used by Asian cybercrime groups. The plugin is used for port forwarding and supports three network commands, each corresponding to an operating mode.
</p>
 <br/>
<div class="table-container">
	<table class="table-article">
	<tbody>
	<tr>
		<td>
			<p>
 <b>Identifier</b>
			</p>
			<b> </b>
		</td>

			<b> </b>
			<p>
				<b>
				Description</b>
			</p>
			<b> </b>
		</td>
	</tr>
	</tbody>
	<tbody>
	<tr>
		<td>
			<p>
				 0х290
			</p>
		</td>
		<td>
			<p>
				 Proxy one request
			</p>
		</td>
	</tr>
	<tr>

			</p>
		</td>
		<td>

			</p>
		</td>
	</tr>
	<tr>
		<td>

			</p>
		</td>
		<td>

			</p>
		</td>
	</tr>
	</tbody>
	</table>
</div>
<br/>
<p>
	Additionally, the main module code contains a reference to a module with the identifier 0xC0, which we did not come across. Apparently, it was a built-in module that executed some actions before the backdoor started.
</p>
<p>
	The configuration header in recent versions looks as follows:
</p>
	<pre>    <div class="code">
struct DeedRATConfigHeader {
    DWORD Signature; // 0xC88CDB32
    BYTE UnusedFlag;
    WORD pInitialKey;
    BYTE PairReplacableFlag1;
    WORD pInstallationPath;
    WORD pSideLoadingDllName;
    WORD pShellcodeName;
    WORD pServiceName;
    WORD pDisplayedServiceName;
    WORD pServiceDescription;
    WORD pPersistentRegistryKey;
    WORD pPersistentRegistryValue;
    BYTE PairReplacableFlag2;
    WORD pTargetProcessForInject1;
    WORD pTargetProcessForInject2;
    WORD pTargetProcessForInject3;
    WORD pTargetProcessForInject4;
    WORD pBotID;
    BYTE UnusedFlag;
    WORD pMutexName;
    BYTE Unknown[58];
    BYTE DayOfWeek1;
    BYTE StartHour1;
    BYTE EndHour1;
    BYTE DayOfWeek2;
    BYTE StartHour2;
    BYTE EndHour2;
    BYTE DayOfWeek3;
    BYTE StartHour3;
    BYTE EndHour3;
    BYTE DayOfWeek4;
    BYTE StartHour4;
    BYTE EndHour4;
    BYTE DnsFlag;
    DWORD DnsIP1;
    DWORD DnsIP2;
    DWORD DnsIP3;
    DWORD DnsIP4;
    BYTE DohFlag;
    WORD pDohAddress1;
    WORD pDohAddress2;
    WORD pDohAddress3;
    WORD pDohAddress4;
    BYTE Unknown[34];
    WORD pC2Url1;
    WORD pC2Url2;
    WORD pC2Url3;
    WORD pC2Url4;
    BYTE UnusedFlag;
    WORD pProxyUrl1;
    WORD pProxyUrl2;
    WORD pProxyUrl3;
    WORD pProxyUrl4;
    BYTE Unknown[3];
};
	    </div>
	</pre>

<p>The rest of the configuration consists of encrypted strings referenced in the header.</p>

<p>The DNS list in the configuration remains unchanged as follows: 8.8.8.8 (Google Public DNS), 1.1.1.1 (Cloudflare DNS), 9.9.9.9 (Quad9 DNS), 222.222.67[.]208. The final DNS likely should be spelled as 208.67.222.222 (Cisco OpenDNS). The config seems to use little-endian addressing, rather than the network byte order. The likely reason why the error might have gone unnoticed so far is that this address is the last one on the list and seldom sees use, while the others are not affected by endianness.</p>

<p>Never once did we see a DNS service hosted at 222.222.67[.]208. We have seen similar attempts to resolve domain names using non-existent DNS servers (see figure below).</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Traffic containing requests to a non-existent DNS server" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/61207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 6. Traffic containing requests to a non-existent DNS server</figcaption> </figure>

<p>Queries like these are a likely sign of Deed RAT infection.</p>

<p>Unlike the sample described above, the backdoor contains the environment pseudovariable %AUTOPATH%, used in the configuration field InstallationPath and, depending on backdoor permissions and system bitness, resolved as follows:</p>

<ul>
<li>%AppData% if the backdoor is missing administrator permissions</li>
<li>%ProgramFiles(x86)% if the backdoor has administrator permissions and the system is 64-bit Windows</li>
<li>%ProgramFiles% if the backdoor has administrator permissions and the system is 32-bit Windows</li>
</ul>

<p>We have seen a similar implementation in PlugX, which used the variable %AUTO%.</p>

<p>
It seems interesting in light of the group’s presumed <a href="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/space-pirates-tools-and-connections/?utm_source=pt&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=space-pirates&amp;utm_content=esc-threat-intelligence#id2" target="_blank">Chinese origins</a> that the number four is a regular feature of the code: four days on which the backdoor cannot run, four links to C&amp;C servers, four links to proxies, four inject processes the malware into, four DNS servers, four DoH addresses. The pronunciation of the Chinese character 四 (four) differs from 死 (death) only in tone, thus the number four is considered unlucky.</p>

<h3 id="id2-2">2.2. Voidoor</h3>

<p>During an investigation, we obtained a sample of unknown, functionally different malware. Our timeline of the sample appearing on the infected computer suggested that the malware is delivered via Deed RAT already installed on the machine and belongs to the Space Pirates group. We were later shown to be right. We named the malware Voidoor, after the C&amp;C server and the backdoor malware type.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Voidoor (ConsoleApplication1.exe) appearing on the infected ALEX-PC computer" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/71207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 7. Voidoor (ConsoleApplication1.exe) appearing on the infected ALEX-PC computer</figcaption> </figure>

<p>Compiled at the end of 2022, Voidoor is a 32-bit EXE file containing the PDB path &quot;C:\_\Project1\Release\Project1.pdb&quot;.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Information from the DIE analysis tool" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/81207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 8. Information from the DIE analysis tool</figcaption> </figure>

<p>Most of the strings inside are XOR-encoded with the key 0×22.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Obfuscated stack strings" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/91207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 9. Obfuscated stack strings</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The Voidoor life cycle can be broken down into the following phases:</p>
<ol>
<li>Preparation</li>
<li>Talking to GitHub repositories</li>
<li>Gaining persistence</li>
<li>Talking to the voidtools forum</li>
<li>Talking to GitHub</li>
</ol>

<h3 id="id2-2-1">2.2.1. Preparatory phase</h3>
<p>The sample starts by trying to open port 27015. If unsuccessful, the process is terminated, so that only one sample is left running at any given time. This is followed by decrypting the bulk of the strings. These can be broken down into the following groups:</p>
<ol>
<li>Talking to GitHub: access token, HTTP headers, user name, user repositories, names of files to be downloaded and run</li>
<li>Talking to the voidtools forum: URI with parameters</li>
<li>Talking to both GitHub and voidtools: User-Agent header: Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 10.0; Win64; x64) AppleWebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/107.0.0.0 Safari/537.36. The HTTP header in the network commands is written in lowercase, unlike the other fields</li>
<li>Local activity: name of scheduler task, paths of files created by Voidoor</li>
</ol>

<p>The above strings are decrypted inside one function, and they can be categorized as file configuration. In addition to that, as the malware runs, it decrypts locally used strings.</p>

<p>This phase also sees Voidoor use the victim identifier, stored in a %TEMP%/ids file. If not present, the file is created, with three concatenated random numbers used as the ID.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Generating a victim ID" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/101207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 10. Generating a victim ID</figcaption> </figure>

<h3 id="id2-2-2">2.2.2. Talking to GitHub repositories</h3>
<p>A personal access token hard-coded in the sample tells us a few things about the owner and their repositories:</p>

	<pre>    <div class="code">
Token issuer: hasdhuahd
Token issuer url: <a href="https://api.github.com/users/hasdhuahd" target="_blank">https://api.github.com/users/hasdhuahd</a>
User created at: 2022-11-23T01:08:24Z
User updated at: 2023-03-20T07:47:54Z

Project:	hasdhuahd/919A1C3FD38A41D89ED53F1967AF443D
Created at:	2022-11-23T03:44:21Z
Visibility:	private

Project:	hasdhuahd/myprivaterepo-1
Created at:	2022-11-23T03:44:32Z
Visibility:	private

Project:	hasdhuahd/13F20E32BDBA46229631517AB130A7E7
Created at:	2022-11-24T04:39:35Z
Visibility:	public

Project:	hasdhuahd/al-khaser
Created at:	2022-12-07T08:16:58Z
Visibility:	public
	    </div>
	</pre>

<ul>
<li>hasdhuahd/919A... acts as the C&amp;C center.</li>
<li>hasdhuahd/myprivaterepo-1 holds the tools used by the malware.</li> 
<li>hasdhuahd/13F2... contains the only file that has a UUID. Its function is unknown.</li>
<li>hasdhuahd/al-khaser is a fork of a public antivirus benchmarking utility.</li>
</ul>

<p>The sample assembles the paths to the repositories it will use.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Building the paths to a repository" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/111207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 11. Building the paths to a repository</figcaption> </figure>

<p>Network communication is handled by libcurl.</p>

<p>Voidoor’s first task is to tell the operators about the new victim. To do this, it builds the link <a href="https://api.github.com/repos/hasdhuahd/919A.../git/trees/main?recursive=1">https://api.github.com/repos/hasdhuahd/919A.../git/trees/main?recursive=1</a> and downloads the file 1A11878899834F1591DFADC277B2132E. If network is unavailable, the program will keep trying until it can download the file. The file maintains a victim list of several dozen strings consisting of a computer name and a pre-generated identifier.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Part of the victim list. The plus sign is used as a delimiter" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/121207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 12. Part of the victim list. The plus sign is used as a delimiter</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The JSON file returned by GitHub is parsed by chopping it into substrings.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Every developer had this phase" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/1312071.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 13. Every developer had this phase</figcaption> </figure>

<p>If the above list does not contain a the identifier generated for the victim, Voidoor sends an HTTP PUT request to api.github.com. GitHub supports adding and modifying files with PUT requests as detailed here: docs.github.com/en/rest/repos/contents#create-or-update-file-contents. Remarkably, this phase includes the decryption of a string in the malware code that will be modified later:</p>

	<pre>    <div class="code">
{&quot;message&quot;: &quot;commit message&quot;, &quot;content&quot;: &quot;dGhpcyBpcyBkb25l&quot;, &quot;sha&quot;: &quot;164adc449d458c4b0819bb348db9b07ca2fc367d&quot;, &quot;branch&quot;: &quot;main&quot;}
	    </div>
	</pre>

<p>The sequence dGhpcyBpcyBkb25l turns into &quot;this is done&quot;. This string is replaced with the ID to be added, and the resulting value is sent to the file 164adc449d458c4b0819bb348db9b07ca2fc367d. The sample then calls the repository myprivaterepo-1, downloading a shellcode file XOR-encrypted with the key 0×22 to the folder %TEMP%\myfile.bin.</p>

<p>It is worth noting that the developer has implemented integrity control by appending a SHA-256 checksum to the end of the file names, which is derived from the downloaded files and checked.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Verifying the checksum of a downloaded file" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/141207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 14. Verifying the checksum of a downloaded file</figcaption> </figure>

<p>Judging by the corrupted shellcode files in the repository history, this desperate measure was intended as an extra guarantee that the file is valid. Interestingly enough, at some point, the developer began to additionally encode binary files in Base64 to avoid byte interpretation issues when storing these in Git.</p>

<p>Then, the sample terminates every process with the name ConsoleApplication1.exe, downloads a file with that name from the tooling repository, and saves it to the folder with the shellcode.</p>

<h3 id="id2-2-3">2.2.3. Gaining persistence</h3>

<p>Voidoor generates a scheduler task as follows:</p>

<p>schtasks /create /tn MyApp /tr &lt;File path&gt; /sc minute /mo 1 /f &amp;&amp; schtasks /create /tn MyApp /tr &lt;File path&gt; /sc minute /mo 1 /ru system /f</p>

<p>This task runs the malware every minute, with system permissions if possible. Clashes that may be caused by this outrageous frequency are avoided by checking port 27015. Notable is the method of gaining persistence: the malware uses the previously downloaded file ConsoleApplication1.exe, which is also used to run the shellcode. The process then generates a task inside the file orderFile.txt, formatting its contents in a way that resembles the output of certutil -encode (see figure below).</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Decrypting stack strings related to certutil" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/151207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 15. Decrypting stack strings related to certutil</figcaption> </figure>

<p>A Base64-encrypted command is placed in the BEGIN CERTIFICATE and END CERTIFICATE strings. The program runs the file ConsoleApplication1, which decrypts the shellcode (using the operation XOR 0×22) and runs it. The file logic is as follows:</p>

	<pre>    <div class="code">
cmd /c certutil -decode C:\Users\Public\Downloads\orderFile.txt C:\Users\Public\Downloads\silentBase.bat &amp;&amp; echo 1 &gt; C:Users\Public\Downloads\checkString || echo 1 &gt; C:\Users\Public\Downloads\checkString
cmd /c type C:\Users\Public\Downloads\silentBase.bat&gt;C:\Users\Public\Downloads\Basesilent.txt &amp;&amp; copy C:\Users\Public\Downloads\Basesilent.txt C:\Users\Public\Downloads\silentBase.bat &amp;&amp; del C:\Users\Public\Downloads\Basesilent.txt &amp;&amp; echo
1&gt;C:\Users\Public\Downloads\checkString || echo 1&gt;C:\Users\Public\Downloads\checkString
cmd /c C:\Users\Public\Downloads\silentBase.bat &amp;&amp;echo 1&gt;C:\Users\Public\Downloads\interResultFile.txt &amp;&amp; echo 1&gt;C:\Users\Public\Downloads\checkString || echo 1&gt;C:\Users\Public\Downloads\checkString
Removal of API files via Windows  C:\Users\Public\Downloads\houston, C:\Users\Public\Downloads\interResultFile.txt, C:\Users\Public\Downloads\silentBase.bat
	    </div>
	</pre>

<p>It can be simplified as follows:</p>

	<pre>    <div class="code">
# Decode orderFile.txt to silentBase.bat
cd C:\Users\Public\Downloads
certutil -decode orderFile.txt silentBase.bat

# Use type and copy commands to complicate automated tracking of links between processes and artifacts
type silentBase.bat&gt;Basesilent.txt
copy Basesilent.txt silentBase.bat
del Basesilent.txt

# Execute the script—in this case, the main file persistence logic
silentBase.bat

# Clean up temporary files
	    </div>
	</pre>

<h3 id="id2-2-4">2.2.4. Talking to the voidtools forum</h3>

<p>To support further operation, the program creates an invisible window with two threads.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Creating two threads" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/161207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 16. Creating two threads</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The second thread serves the simple purpose of standing by for ten hours, then activating the termination flag for the first one.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Body of the termination control thread" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/171207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 17. Body of the termination control thread</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The flag will be checked in the global cycle of the first thread.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Global cycle of the first thread with the exit condition" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/181207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 18. Global cycle of the first thread with the exit condition</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The checks relating to the forum part must be passed to proceed to the next phase.</p>

<p>First, the thread decrypts the strings <a href="https://www.voidtools.com">https://www.voidtools.com</a>, /forum/ucp.php, and ?i=ucp_pm&amp;mode=options. &quot;UCP&quot; means &quot;User Control Panel&quot; in the context of this website. Interestingly, the sample adds &quot;asdasdasd&quot; to the cookie request header, but we could not find any common sense in that.</p>

<p>The process concatenates the strings and sends a request to the resulting address. If there is a connection, the request will be redirected to the login page.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Forum login form" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/191207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 19. Forum login form</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The sample will then send a POST request to log in to the forum using the hard-coded login and password, and if successful, store the values of the phpbb3_h6rei_u, phpbb3_h6rei_k, and phpbb3_h6rei_sid cookies, which are required for the session.</p>

<p>The forum has a personal messaging system that supports custom rules.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Email rules from several malware samples" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/201207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 20. Email rules from several malware samples</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The sample will try to define a new rule even if this rule already exists:</p>
<p>check_option=1&amp;rule_option=1&amp;rule_string=^&lt;victim ID&gt;^&amp;rule_user_id=0&amp;rule_group_id=0&amp;cond_option=text&amp;action_option=1|0&amp;add_rule=Add rule&amp;foldername=&amp;rename_folder_id=8&amp;new_folder_name=&amp;remove_folder_id=8&amp;remove_action=1&amp;move_to=0&amp;full_move_to=0&amp;full_action=3&amp;creation_time=&lt;device timestamp&gt;&amp;form_token=&lt;parsed token from the page&gt;<br/></p>

 <figure> <img alt="Warning message when trying to create a duplicate rule" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/211207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 21. Warning message when trying to create a duplicate rule</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The malware will download the page with the list of rules again. This time, though, it is looking for a folder whose name features the victim ID.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="List of directories and folders" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/221207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 22. List of directories and folders</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The folder must be created by the C&amp;C server, or else the sample will get stuck in a loop for ten hours repeatedly adding the new rule. Multiple folders cannot be created, as the sample will take the first entry for comparison. We suspect this means that the C&amp;C server can communicate with only one sample via GitHub at any given time.</p>

<p>The forum is powered by the phpBB engine; it proved to be a treasure trove of useful information.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Account registration date" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/231207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 23. Account registration date</figcaption> </figure>

 <figure> <img alt="Address created by a temporary email service" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/241207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 24. Address created by a temporary email service</figcaption> </figure>

 <figure> <img alt="The time zone is Antarctic" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/251207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 25. The time zone is Antarctic</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The forum notably requires some activity from users before allowing them to send email.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Restriction on messaging for newcomers" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/261207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 26. Restriction on messaging for newcomers</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The so-called &quot;Remember me&quot; login keys were a real catch. This function helps to manage active sessions whose tokens are stored client-side. If the device is stolen, the user can block it by removing the key from the list. The device will lose access to the profile, and the forum will ask for a user name and password to log in again. This is a legacy feature based on a use case that was described in a 2004 post we found on the phpBB community forum. We consider that functionality to be dangerous.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Top of the active session list" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/271207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 27. Top of the active session list</figcaption> </figure>

<p>We found more than 3,500 login events associated with 73 unique IP addresses, and we were able to attribute voidoor to the APT group after discovering a series of logins from Space Pirates IP addresses that occurred within days of registering the account. By correlating these events with activities in the GitHub repository, we established that these logins took place during the malware development and testing phases.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Addresses related to the Space Pirates C&amp;C server" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/281207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 28. Addresses related to the Space Pirates C&amp;C server</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The hackers have targeted universities, healthcare centers, energy companies, private security providers and government organizations in Russia and Serbia.</p>

<h3 id="id2-2-5">2.2.5. GitHub-based C&amp;C server</h3>

<p>The sample switches to the communication mode based on GitHub commands. It searches the repository 919A... for a file whose name consists of two parts: a string of the same type as the value returned by the command and an identifier.</p>

<p>Communication takes place as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>The malware receives a command in the specified file. The command consists of three strings: the command identifier, the return value type, and the command body. We are aware of the following two return value types:</li>
<ul>
<li>D737C9A763E941BDAA69C6EE83553014: download the file from the victim’s computer and upload it to GitHub</li>
<li>139445A83B5B4ED79FAF4439FC7FFE69: execute the command</li>
</ul>
<li>The sample runs the above task and uses a PUT request to upload an object with the name formatted as &lt;command type&gt; + &lt;victim identifier&gt; to the repository.<br/></li>
<li>The process loops to the start: the sample returns to standby mode, waiting to get a command with an identifier different from the previous one.</li>
</ol>
<p>Example of this kind of communication:</p>

	<pre>    <div class="code">
datetime: 2022-11-24 12:40:59+08:00
message: commit message
1A11878899834F1591DFADC277B2132E 2 insertions, 0 deletions, 2 lines (file with the new infected victim added)
&gt;&gt;&gt;
\n
DESKTOP-94KT1VQ+200882088117246
&lt;&lt;&lt;

datetime: 2022-11-24 12:42:05+08:00
message: commit message
D7B3FDC2EABE453BB39FA73557FC77F3200882088117246 4 insertions, 0 deletions, 4 lines 
&gt;&gt;&gt; 
uuid: 8b0e4a01-b242-45a4-a86d-25ab54a3308a
md5: 139445A83B5B4ED79FAF4439FC7FFE69
cmd: hostname
&lt;&lt;&lt;

datetime: 2022-11-24 12:46:30+08:00
message: commit message
A2EE1A74A32344FEA87A42D395013499200882088117246 5 insertions, 0 deletions, 5 lines
&gt;&gt;&gt; GB18030 (simplified_chinese):

C:\mylittletrojan\shellcodeloader\thumb_drive-main\thumb_drive_copy_real_time\7z2200-src\CPP\7zip\UI\Client7z&gt;hostname
DESKTOP-94KT1VQ

&lt;&lt;&lt;
	    </div>
	</pre>

<p>Unfortunately, our copy of the file is missing that functionality: the command identifier includes an extraneous hard-coded identifier with an unknown return value type: D7B3FDC2EABE453BB39FA73557FC77F3171542571331346. The string prevents the code from executing correctly, causing the sample to loop for ten hours, as the termination flag that the cycle checks is set by the second thread. As the string is XOR-encrypted in its entirety inside the file, the function can be considered deactivated but not removed.</p>

<h3 id="id2-2-6">2.2.6. Some facts about the developer of the tool</h3>

<p>By analyzing the GitHub repositories, we can easily identify the testing and operation phases of the malware. We know that the name of the hacker’s device is desktop-94kt1vq. Online search returns a blog on Chinese Software Developer Network.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Web search results" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/291207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 29. Web search results</figcaption> </figure>

 <figure> <img alt="Developer profile" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/301207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 30. Developer profile</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The user posts a lot, with a total of 177 original entries, and importantly for us, his name in the system (&quot;X&quot;) matches the name used by the C&amp;C server.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="The user name X and the default project name ConsoleApplication" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/311207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 31. The user name &quot;X&quot; and the default project name &quot;ConsoleApplication&quot;</figcaption> </figure>

<p>Some of the user&#x27;s other noteworthy blog posts deal with storing files on GitHub, using IDA Pro and reverse engineering in general, and kernel programming.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Post on storing files on GitHub" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/321207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 32. Post on storing files on GitHub</figcaption> </figure>

<p>The profile description caught our eyes too.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Description of the first account" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/331207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 33. Description of the first account</figcaption> </figure>

<p>This mentions another account, abandoned in March 2021.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Second account" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/341207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 34. Second account</figcaption> </figure>

<p>This other blog focuses mostly on pentesting, vulnerability analysis, and descriptions of internal Windows mechanics.</p>

<p>By comparing these pieces of information (matching computer names, user names, and relevant skills), we can assume with some confidence that this person is one of the developers of the malware in question, if not the only one.</p>

<h3 id="id2-3">2.3. Other tools</h3>

<p>Besides the backdoors described above, the hackers have made use of the following publicly available network tools:</p>

<ul>
<li>Stowaway</li>
<li>Mimikatz</li>
<li>fscan</li>
<li>procdump</li>
<li>PortQry версии 2.0 Gold</li>
<li>NetSess</li>
<li>NBTscan</li>
<li>PsExec</li>
<li>KrbRelayUp</li>
<li>SharpRoast</li>
<li>nmap</li>
<li>Impacket</li>
<li>CHAOS</li>
<li>reGeorg</li>
<li>Neo-reGeorg</li>
<li>Godzilla (web shell)</li>
<li>xсmdsvc</li>
</ul>

<p>The group often uses tools written in Golang and obfuscated with Garble. We also found a homebrew utility that is not available publicly and likely has been developed by the Space Pirates group. It monitors connected drives, collecting files from every newly appearing device and creating a new database record. The utility uses the 7z.dll library to pack files into an archive with a name formatted as hh.mm.ss, where hh is the current hour, mm is the current minute, and ss is the current second. All archives are saved to C:\Users\Public\Downloads\dest.</p>

<p>The utility uses two database files: 1.db in place of mutexes and 2.db for logging connected devices. Information about the latest changes to the removable drive contents is stored here as well, so the utility can check if it needs to copy any new files. The program masquerades as the 7-Zip file archiver.</p>

 <figure> <img alt="Properties of the removable-drive monitoring utility" loading="lazy" width="0" height="0" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="HtmlContent_image-next__g1zfK" style="color:transparent" src="https://www.ptsecurity.com/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/351207.png"/> <figcaption>Figure 35. Properties of the removable-drive monitoring utility</figcaption> </figure>

<h2 id="id3">Conclusion</h2>

<p>The Space Pirates group is relentlessly stepping up activity targeting Russian companies: the number of attacks has increased manifold. The hackers are working on new malware that implements unconventional techniques, such as voidoor, and modifying their existing malware. In addition, we have seen a drastic reduction in the use of other backdoors characteristic of the group and an increase in attacks that employ Deed RAT.</p>

<p>The Space Pirates group uses a large number of publicly available tools for navigating networks. The hackers also use Acunetix to reconnoiter infrastructures it targets. Meanwhile, the group’s tactics have hardly changed.</p>

<p>The cybercriminals’ main goals are still espionage and theft of confidential information, but the group has expanded its interests and the geography of its attacks.</p>

<p>The PT ESC team continues to monitor and respond to threats, including those associated with the Space Pirates group.</p>

<p><b>Authors:</b> Denis Kuvshinov, Stanislav Rakovsky, Stanislav Pyzhov</p>

Deed RAT\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n        \u003cli\u003e\u003ca class=\"link link_article-nav anchorLink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"#id2-2\"\u003e2.2. Voidoor\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cul\u003e\r\n        \u003cli\u003e\u003ca class=\"link link_article-nav anchorLink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"#id2-2-1\"\u003e2.2.1. Preparatory phase\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n        \u003cli\u003e\u003ca class=\"link link_article-nav anchorLink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"#id2-2-2\"\u003e2.2.2. Talking to GitHub repositories\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n        \u003cli\u003e\u003ca class=\"link link_article-nav anchorLink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"#id2-2-3\"\u003e2.2.3. Gaining persistence\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n        \u003cli\u003e\u003ca class=\"link link_article-nav anchorLink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"#id2-2-4\"\u003e2.2.4. Talking to the voidtools forum\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n        \u003cli\u003e\u003ca class=\"link link_article-nav anchorLink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"#id2-2-5\"\u003e2.2.5. GitHub-based C\u0026amp;C server\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n        \u003cli\u003e\u003ca class=\"link link_article-nav anchorLink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"#id2-2-6\"\u003e2.2.6. Some facts about the developer of the tool\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n        \u003cli\u003e\u003ca class=\"link link_article-nav anchorLink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"#id2-3\"\u003e2.3. Other tools\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n        \u003cli\u003e\u003ca class=\"link link_article-nav anchorLink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"#id3\"\u003eConclusion\u003c/a\u003e\r\n        \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003ca class=\"link link_article-nav anchorLink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"#id4\"\u003eApplications\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\u003c/div\u003e\r\n\u003carticle\u003e\r\n\u003ch2 id=\"id0\"\u003eIntroduction\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\nAt\u0026nbsp;the end of\u0026nbsp;2019, the team at\u0026nbsp;the Positive Technologies Expert Security Center (PT\u0026nbsp;ESC) discovered a\u0026nbsp;new cybercrime group, which they dubbed Space Pirates. It\u0026nbsp;had been active since at\u0026nbsp;least 2017. The first-ever comprehensive \u003ca href=\"https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/space-pirates-tools-and-connections/?utm_source=pt\u0026utm_medium=article\u0026utm_campaign=space-pirates\u0026utm_content=esc-threat-intelligence\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eresearch paper\u003c/a\u003e describing the group saw light in\u0026nbsp;early 2022. The Space Pirates group have since stepped up\u0026nbsp;attacks on\u0026nbsp;Russian companies: we\u0026nbsp;have come across the group frequently while investigating cyberattacks in\u0026nbsp;the past year. They have hardly changed their tactics, but they have developed new tools and improved their old ones.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe cybercriminals’ main goals are still espionage and theft of\u0026nbsp;confidential information, but the group has expanded its interests and the geography of\u0026nbsp;its attacks. Over the year, at\u0026nbsp;least 16\u0026nbsp;organizations have been attacked in\u0026nbsp;Russia and one in\u0026nbsp;Serbia. Some of\u0026nbsp;the new victims that we\u0026nbsp;identified are Russian and Serbian government and educational institutions, private security companies, aerospace manufacturers, agricultural producers, defense, energy, and infosec companies.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2 id=\"id1\"\u003e1. Investigating the network infrastructure\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t We\u0026nbsp;found an\u0026nbsp;Acunetix installation on\u0026nbsp;one of\u0026nbsp;the Space Pirates command-and-control (C\u0026amp;C) servers, which suggested that the group exploited vulnerabilities—an attack vector we\u0026nbsp;had not seen it\u0026nbsp;use earlier.\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"Evidence of Acunetix being installed on a Space Pirates C\u0026amp;C server\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/11207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 1. Evidence of Acunetix being installed on a Space Pirates C\u0026amp;C server\u003c/figcaption\u003e \u003c/figure\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tDuring our investigation, we\u0026nbsp;noticed that the group was interested in\u0026nbsp;PST email archives (among other targets). A\u0026nbsp;configuration error on\u0026nbsp;a\u0026nbsp;Space Pirates C\u0026amp;C server allowed\u0026nbsp;us to\u0026nbsp;scan its contents, discovering two email archives belonging to\u0026nbsp;a\u0026nbsp;Serbian ministry.\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"C\u0026amp;C server with web shells and stolen data\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/21207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 2. C\u0026amp;C server with web shells and stolen data\u003c/figcaption\u003e \u003c/figure\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tWe\u0026nbsp;alerted the ministry via Serbia’s National CERT. Other contents of\u0026nbsp;the server included a\u0026nbsp;Godzilla web shell and an\u0026nbsp;obfuscated Neo-reGeorg tunnel.\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tThe Space Pirates network infrastructure continues to\u0026nbsp;use a\u0026nbsp;small number of\u0026nbsp;IP addresses as\u0026nbsp;indicated by\u0026nbsp;the DDNS domains. The malicious actors often reuse old website URLs by\u0026nbsp;creating high-level domains, such as\u0026nbsp;ruclient.dns04.com.ruclient.dns04.com.\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tThe group had also begun using the ShadowPad malware, something we\u0026nbsp;discovered as\u0026nbsp;we\u0026nbsp;were tracking changes in\u0026nbsp;the hacker infrastructure using our internal ScanDat automated system. An\u0026nbsp;alert we\u0026nbsp;received pointed to\u0026nbsp;a\u0026nbsp;chain of\u0026nbsp;SSL certificates characteristic of\u0026nbsp;ShadowPad. That chain was covered in\u0026nbsp;one of\u0026nbsp;our previous \u003ca href=\"https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/shadowpad-new-activity-from-the-winnti-group/?utm_source=pt\u0026utm_medium=article\u0026utm_campaign=space-pirates\u0026utm_content=esc-threat-intelligence\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ereports\u003c/a\u003e. As\u0026nbsp;we\u0026nbsp;continued to\u0026nbsp;investigate the incident in\u0026nbsp;question, we\u0026nbsp;found a\u0026nbsp;copy of\u0026nbsp;ShadowPad used by\u0026nbsp;the Space Pirates group in\u0026nbsp;the client’s systems.\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"Chain of SSL certificates characteristic of ShadowPad\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/31207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 3. Chain of SSL certificates characteristic of ShadowPad\u003c/figcaption\u003e \u003c/figure\u003e\r\n\u003ch2 id=\"id2\"\u003e2. Analysis of\u0026nbsp;the malware and tools\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\u003ch3 id=\"id2-1\"\u003e2.1. Deed RAT\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tVirtually every investigation we\u0026nbsp;conducted found that the group was using Deed RAT. As\u0026nbsp;far as\u0026nbsp;we\u0026nbsp;can tell, the Space Pirates group is\u0026nbsp;moving away from other backdoors. Code similarities between Deed RAT and ShadowPad, noted by\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://xakep.ru/2022/07/28/chinese-malware/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eour peers\u003c/a\u003e, suggest that the backdoor is\u0026nbsp;an\u0026nbsp;evolution of\u0026nbsp;ShadowPad. ShadowPad is\u0026nbsp;in\u0026nbsp;turn believed to\u0026nbsp;be\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://habr.com/ru/companies/drweb/articles/525086/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ean\u0026nbsp;evolution of\u0026nbsp;PlugX\u003c/a\u003e. Unlike ShadowPad and PlugX, though, Deed RAT has been known to\u0026nbsp;be\u0026nbsp;exclusive to\u0026nbsp;the Space Pirates group to\u0026nbsp;date.\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tThe backdoor is\u0026nbsp;still under active development. We\u0026nbsp;found a\u0026nbsp;64-bit version of\u0026nbsp;Deed RAT on\u0026nbsp;an\u0026nbsp;infected device while investigating the incident. The structure of\u0026nbsp;the main module and plugin headers is\u0026nbsp;all but identical to\u0026nbsp;the 32-bit version. Below is\u0026nbsp;what it\u0026nbsp;looks like:\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\u003cpre\u003e    \u003cdiv class=\"code\"\u003e\r\nstruct SectionHeader {\r\n    DWORD VirtualSize;\r\n    DWORD SizeOfRawData;\r\n};\r\n\r\nstruct ModuleHeader {\r\n    DWORD Signature; // 0xDEED4554\r\n    DWORD ModuleId;\r\n    DWORD EntryPoint;\r\n    QWORD OriginalBase;\r\n    DWORD AbsoluteOffset;\r\n    SectionHeader Sections[3];\r\n    DWORD RelocationsVirtualSize;\r\n};\r\n\t    \u003c/div\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tThe string encryption algorithm in\u0026nbsp;recent versions is\u0026nbsp;somewhat different. String length is\u0026nbsp;no\u0026nbsp;longer specified, and strings are null-terminated.\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"Original encryption algorithm, with string length explicitly stated\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/41207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 4. Original encryption algorithm, with string length explicitly stated\u003c/figcaption\u003e \u003c/figure\u003e \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"Updated decryption algorithm for null-terminated strings\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/51207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 5. Updated decryption algorithm for null-terminated strings\u003c/figcaption\u003e \u003c/figure\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tWe\u0026nbsp;found computers infected with Deed RAT to\u0026nbsp;contain two plugins, retrieved dynamically from the C\u0026amp;C\u0026nbsp;server. The first one is\u0026nbsp;named Disk, has the identifier 0×250, and is\u0026nbsp;used as\u0026nbsp;a\u0026nbsp;disk tool. Essentially a\u0026nbsp;Windows API wrapper, Disk supports the 10\u0026nbsp;network commands described below.\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n \u003cbr\u003e\r\n\u003cdiv class=\"table-container\"\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctable class=\"table-article\"\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctbody\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n \u003cb\u003eIdentifier\u003c/b\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cb\u003e \u003c/b\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cb\u003e \u003c/b\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t\u003cb\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\tDescription\u003c/b\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cb\u003e \u003c/b\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tbody\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctbody\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t 0х250\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t List disks\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t 0х251\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t List files inside folder\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t 0х252\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t List files inside folder recursively. resources\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t 0х25B\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t Connect network drive. The command sends a\u0026nbsp;NETRESOURCEW structure\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tbody\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/table\u003e\r\n\u003c/div\u003e\r\n\u003cbr\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tThe other plugin is\u0026nbsp;named Portmap and has the identifier 0×290. The hackers likely based it\u0026nbsp;on\u0026nbsp;the \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/kufan/LViewer/blob/master/Client/ZXPortMap.cpp\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eZXPortMap\u003c/a\u003e utility often used by\u0026nbsp;Asian cybercrime groups. The plugin is\u0026nbsp;used for port forwarding and supports three network commands, each corresponding to\u0026nbsp;an\u0026nbsp;operating mode.\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n \u003cbr\u003e\r\n\u003cdiv class=\"table-container\"\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctable class=\"table-article\"\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctbody\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n \u003cb\u003eIdentifier\u003c/b\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cb\u003e \u003c/b\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cb\u003e \u003c/b\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t\u003cb\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\tDescription\u003c/b\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cb\u003e \u003c/b\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tbody\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctbody\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t 0х290\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t Proxy one request\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t 0х292\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t Start simple proxy on\u0026nbsp;specified port\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t 0х294\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\t Start SOCKS5 proxy without authentication on\u0026nbsp;specified port\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tbody\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/table\u003e\r\n\u003c/div\u003e\r\n\u003cbr\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tAdditionally, the main module code contains a\u0026nbsp;reference to\u0026nbsp;a\u0026nbsp;module with the identifier 0xC0, which we\u0026nbsp;did not come across. Apparently, it\u0026nbsp;was a\u0026nbsp;built-in module that executed some actions before the backdoor started.\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\n\tThe configuration header in\u0026nbsp;recent versions looks as\u0026nbsp;follows:\r\n\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\u003cpre\u003e    \u003cdiv class=\"code\"\u003e\r\nstruct DeedRATConfigHeader {\r\n    DWORD Signature; // 0xC88CDB32\r\n    BYTE UnusedFlag;\r\n    WORD pInitialKey;\r\n    BYTE PairReplacableFlag1;\r\n    WORD pInstallationPath;\r\n    WORD pSideLoadingDllName;\r\n    WORD pShellcodeName;\r\n    WORD pServiceName;\r\n    WORD pDisplayedServiceName;\r\n    WORD pServiceDescription;\r\n    WORD pPersistentRegistryKey;\r\n    WORD pPersistentRegistryValue;\r\n    BYTE PairReplacableFlag2;\r\n    WORD pTargetProcessForInject1;\r\n    WORD pTargetProcessForInject2;\r\n    WORD pTargetProcessForInject3;\r\n    WORD pTargetProcessForInject4;\r\n    WORD pBotID;\r\n    BYTE UnusedFlag;\r\n    WORD pMutexName;\r\n    BYTE Unknown[58];\r\n    BYTE DayOfWeek1;\r\n    BYTE StartHour1;\r\n    BYTE EndHour1;\r\n    BYTE DayOfWeek2;\r\n    BYTE StartHour2;\r\n    BYTE EndHour2;\r\n    BYTE DayOfWeek3;\r\n    BYTE StartHour3;\r\n    BYTE EndHour3;\r\n    BYTE DayOfWeek4;\r\n    BYTE StartHour4;\r\n    BYTE EndHour4;\r\n    BYTE DnsFlag;\r\n    DWORD DnsIP1;\r\n    DWORD DnsIP2;\r\n    DWORD DnsIP3;\r\n    DWORD DnsIP4;\r\n    BYTE DohFlag;\r\n    WORD pDohAddress1;\r\n    WORD pDohAddress2;\r\n    WORD pDohAddress3;\r\n    WORD pDohAddress4;\r\n    BYTE Unknown[34];\r\n    WORD pC2Url1;\r\n    WORD pC2Url2;\r\n    WORD pC2Url3;\r\n    WORD pC2Url4;\r\n    BYTE UnusedFlag;\r\n    WORD pProxyUrl1;\r\n    WORD pProxyUrl2;\r\n    WORD pProxyUrl3;\r\n    WORD pProxyUrl4;\r\n    BYTE Unknown[3];\r\n};\r\n\t    \u003c/div\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe rest of\u0026nbsp;the configuration consists of\u0026nbsp;encrypted strings referenced in\u0026nbsp;the header.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe DNS list in\u0026nbsp;the configuration remains unchanged as\u0026nbsp;follows: 8.8.8.8 (Google Public DNS), 1.1.1.1 (Cloudflare DNS), 9.9.9.9 (Quad9\u0026nbsp;DNS), 222.222.67[.]208. The final DNS likely should be\u0026nbsp;spelled as\u0026nbsp;208.67.222.222 (Cisco OpenDNS). The config seems to\u0026nbsp;use little-endian addressing, rather than the network byte order. The likely reason why the error might have gone unnoticed so\u0026nbsp;far is\u0026nbsp;that this address is\u0026nbsp;the last one on\u0026nbsp;the list and seldom sees use, while the others are not affected by\u0026nbsp;endianness.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eNever once did we\u0026nbsp;see a\u0026nbsp;DNS service hosted at\u0026nbsp;222.222.67[.]208. We\u0026nbsp;have seen similar attempts to\u0026nbsp;resolve domain names using non-existent DNS servers (see figure below).\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"Traffic containing requests to a non-existent DNS server\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/61207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 6. Traffic containing requests to a non-existent DNS server\u003c/figcaption\u003e \u003c/figure\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eQueries like these are a\u0026nbsp;likely sign of\u0026nbsp;Deed RAT infection.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eUnlike the sample described above, the backdoor contains the environment pseudovariable %AUTOPATH%, used in\u0026nbsp;the configuration field InstallationPath and, depending on\u0026nbsp;backdoor permissions and system bitness, resolved as\u0026nbsp;follows:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003e%AppData% if\u0026nbsp;the backdoor is\u0026nbsp;missing administrator permissions\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003e%ProgramFiles(x86)% if\u0026nbsp;the backdoor has administrator permissions and the system is\u0026nbsp;64-bit Windows\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003e%ProgramFiles% if\u0026nbsp;the backdoor has administrator permissions and the system is\u0026nbsp;32-bit Windows\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWe\u0026nbsp;have seen a\u0026nbsp;similar implementation in\u0026nbsp;PlugX, which used the variable %AUTO%.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\r\nIt\u0026nbsp;seems interesting in\u0026nbsp;light of\u0026nbsp;the group’s presumed \u003ca href=\"https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/space-pirates-tools-and-connections/?utm_source=pt\u0026utm_medium=article\u0026utm_campaign=space-pirates\u0026utm_content=esc-threat-intelligence#id2\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eChinese origins\u003c/a\u003e that the number four is\u0026nbsp;a\u0026nbsp;regular feature of\u0026nbsp;the code: four days on\u0026nbsp;which the backdoor cannot run, four links to\u0026nbsp;C\u0026amp;C\u0026nbsp;servers, four links to\u0026nbsp;proxies, four inject processes the malware into, four DNS servers, four DoH addresses. The pronunciation of\u0026nbsp;the Chinese character 四 (four) differs from 死 (death) only in\u0026nbsp;tone, thus the number four is\u0026nbsp;considered unlucky.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3 id=\"id2-2\"\u003e2.2. Voidoor\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eDuring an\u0026nbsp;investigation, we\u0026nbsp;obtained a\u0026nbsp;sample of\u0026nbsp;unknown, functionally different malware. Our timeline of\u0026nbsp;the sample appearing on\u0026nbsp;the infected computer suggested that the malware is\u0026nbsp;delivered via Deed RAT already installed on\u0026nbsp;the machine and belongs to\u0026nbsp;the Space Pirates group. We\u0026nbsp;were later shown to\u0026nbsp;be\u0026nbsp;right. We\u0026nbsp;named the malware Voidoor, after the C\u0026amp;C server and the backdoor malware type.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"Voidoor (ConsoleApplication1.exe) appearing on the infected ALEX-PC computer\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/71207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 7. Voidoor (ConsoleApplication1.exe) appearing on the infected ALEX-PC computer\u003c/figcaption\u003e \u003c/figure\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eCompiled at\u0026nbsp;the end of\u0026nbsp;2022, Voidoor is\u0026nbsp;a\u0026nbsp;32-bit EXE file containing the PDB path \"C:\\_\\Project1\\Release\\Project1.pdb\".\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"Information from the DIE analysis tool\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/81207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 8. Information from the DIE analysis tool\u003c/figcaption\u003e \u003c/figure\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eMost of\u0026nbsp;the strings inside are XOR-encoded with the key 0×22.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"Obfuscated stack strings\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/91207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 9. Obfuscated stack strings\u003c/figcaption\u003e \u003c/figure\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe Voidoor life cycle can be\u0026nbsp;broken down into the following phases:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003col\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003ePreparation\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003eTalking to\u0026nbsp;GitHub repositories\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003eGaining persistence\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003eTalking to\u0026nbsp;the voidtools forum\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003eTalking to\u0026nbsp;GitHub\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ol\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3 id=\"id2-2-1\"\u003e2.2.1. Preparatory phase\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe sample starts by\u0026nbsp;trying to\u0026nbsp;open port 27015. If\u0026nbsp;unsuccessful, the process is\u0026nbsp;terminated, so\u0026nbsp;that only one sample is\u0026nbsp;left running at\u0026nbsp;any given time. This is\u0026nbsp;followed by\u0026nbsp;decrypting the bulk of\u0026nbsp;the strings. These can be\u0026nbsp;broken down into the following groups:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003col\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003eTalking to\u0026nbsp;GitHub: access token, HTTP headers, user name, user repositories, names of\u0026nbsp;files to\u0026nbsp;be\u0026nbsp;downloaded and run\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003eTalking to\u0026nbsp;the voidtools forum: URI with parameters\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003eTalking to\u0026nbsp;both GitHub and voidtools: User-Agent header: Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT\u0026nbsp;10.0; Win64; x64) AppleWebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/107.0.0.0\u0026nbsp;Safari/537.36. The HTTP header in\u0026nbsp;the network commands is\u0026nbsp;written in\u0026nbsp;lowercase, unlike the other fields\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003eLocal activity: name of\u0026nbsp;scheduler task, paths of\u0026nbsp;files created by\u0026nbsp;Voidoor\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ol\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe above strings are decrypted inside one function, and they can be\u0026nbsp;categorized as\u0026nbsp;file configuration. In\u0026nbsp;addition to\u0026nbsp;that, as\u0026nbsp;the malware runs, it\u0026nbsp;decrypts locally used strings.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThis phase also sees Voidoor use the victim identifier, stored in\u0026nbsp;a\u0026nbsp;%TEMP%/ids file. If\u0026nbsp;not present, the file is\u0026nbsp;created, with three concatenated random numbers used as\u0026nbsp;the\u0026nbsp;ID.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"Generating a victim ID\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/101207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 10. Generating a victim ID\u003c/figcaption\u003e \u003c/figure\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3 id=\"id2-2-2\"\u003e2.2.2. Talking to\u0026nbsp;GitHub repositories\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eA\u0026nbsp;personal access token hard-coded in\u0026nbsp;the sample tells\u0026nbsp;us a\u0026nbsp;few things about the owner and their repositories:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\t\u003cpre\u003e    \u003cdiv class=\"code\"\u003e\r\nToken issuer: hasdhuahd\r\nToken issuer url: \u003ca href=\"https://api.github.com/users/hasdhuahd\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ehttps://api.github.com/users/hasdhuahd\u003c/a\u003e\r\nUser created at: 2022-11-23T01:08:24Z\r\nUser updated at: 2023-03-20T07:47:54Z\r\n\r\nProject:\thasdhuahd/919A1C3FD38A41D89ED53F1967AF443D\r\nCreated at:\t2022-11-23T03:44:21Z\r\nVisibility:\tprivate\r\n\r\nProject:\thasdhuahd/myprivaterepo-1\r\nCreated at:\t2022-11-23T03:44:32Z\r\nVisibility:\tprivate\r\n\r\nProject:\thasdhuahd/13F20E32BDBA46229631517AB130A7E7\r\nCreated at:\t2022-11-24T04:39:35Z\r\nVisibility:\tpublic\r\n\r\nProject:\thasdhuahd/al-khaser\r\nCreated at:\t2022-12-07T08:16:58Z\r\nVisibility:\tpublic\r\n\t    \u003c/div\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003ehasdhuahd/919A... acts as\u0026nbsp;the C\u0026amp;C center.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003ehasdhuahd/myprivaterepo-1 holds the tools used by\u0026nbsp;the malware.\u003c/li\u003e \r\n\u003cli\u003ehasdhuahd/13F2... contains the only file that has a\u0026nbsp;UUID. Its function is\u0026nbsp;unknown.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003cli\u003ehasdhuahd/al-khaser is\u0026nbsp;a\u0026nbsp;fork of\u0026nbsp;a\u0026nbsp;public antivirus benchmarking utility.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe sample assembles the paths to\u0026nbsp;the repositories it\u0026nbsp;will use.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n \u003cfigure\u003e \u003cimg alt=\"Building the paths to a repository\" src=\"/upload/corporate/ru-ru/images/pt-esc/space-pirates/111207.png\"\u003e \u003cfigcaption\u003eFigure 11.
